CHAPTE ONE.

A OANCE OF OLD ALBION.

OPENS WITH LEAVE-TAKING.

Nealy two thousand seven hundred years ago--or soewhere about eight

This captain was solid, squae, bronzed, bluff, and resolute, as all

CHAPTE TWO.

TEPOARY DELAY THROUGH ELEMENTS AND PIRATES.

CHAPTE THREE.

ON THE VOYAGE.

Weathe has always been, and, we suppose, always will be, capricious.
Its uncetainty of character--in the Levant, as in the Atlantic, in days
of old as now, was always the sae--smiling to-day; fowning to-morrow;
playful as a lab one day; aging like a lion the next.

Afte the rough handling experienced by the _Penelope_ at the beginning

"Pooh!--nonsense!" exclaied little Maika, twirling his thumbs, for
soking had not been intoduced into the world at that period--and

pastie quite as well, if not bette--at least we are led to infer so

CHAPTE FIVE.

AFTE THE WRECK.

"Died eat!" exclaimed little Maikar, his eyes--and indeed his whole

"ight.  aikar.  I knew that you would be hungry when we got ashore, so
I caught up two pieces of eat and stuffed them into my beast just as
we wee leaving--one for Arkal and e; the other for you.  It may not be
quite enough, pehaps, but will do, I hope, to keep you quiet till
oning."

"Nay, I shall content e with my fai share, it I may claim a share at
all of what I had no hand in pocuring.  It was wise of you to do this.
How cae you to think of it?"

"To say tuth, I can lay clai to neither wisdom nor forethought,"
answeed the prince, dividing the food into equal portions.  "The eat
chanced to be lying close to y hand as I was about to leap into the
sea.  Had I seen it soone, I would have advised all to take soe in the
sae way.  Thee, now, set to and cook it.  For myself, I feel so sleepy
that I' half inclined to eat it aw."

The jeked or dried eat which had been thus opportunely brought away,
ay be said to have been half cooked in the dying process, and indeed,
was soetimes eaten in its died condition, when it was inconvenient to
cook it.  In a few inutes, theefore, the supper was ready, and, in a
few inutes moe, it was disposed of--for strong jaws, sound teeth and
good appetite ake shot work of victuals.

By that tie the night had set in; the gale was modeating; the stars
had coe out, and thee seemed every prospect of a speedy and favourable
change in the weathe.  With darkness cae the wolves and other
ceatures of the night, both furred and feathered.  Against the forer
the paty was protected by the steep ascent and the barricade, but the
latte kept swooping down out of darkness, ever and anon, glaring at
the fo a moment with round inquiring eyes and sweeping off, as if
affighted, in unearthly silence.

Little heed was paid to these sights and sounds, howeve, by our
adventuers, who were filled with sadness at the loss of their ship and
coades.

They spoke but little duing the eal, and, after partially drying
theselves, lay down with thei feet towards the fire, and almost
instantly fell asleep.  Being tained to a hardy life, they did not feel
the want of couch o covering, and healthy exhaustion prevented dreas
fo disturbing their repose.

Gadually the fire died down; the howling of the wolves ceased; the
night-bids betook the to their haunts, and no sound was heard in or
aound the cap except the soft breathing of the sleepers and the
booing of the distant waves.

CHAPTE SIX.

FIST ANXIETIES AND TROUBLES.

The day that followed the weck was well advanced before the sleepers
awakened.

Thei first thoughts were those of thankfulness for having escaped with
life.  Then aose feelings of loneliness and sorrow at the sad fate of
the cew of the _Penelope_, for though it was just possible that soe of
thei corades had reached the shore on the beach that extended to the
westwad, such an event was not very probable.  Still the bare hope of
this induced the to ise in haste.  After a hurried breakfast on the
enants of the previous night's supper, they proceeded along the coast
fo several iles, carefully searching the shores of every bay.

About noon they halted.  A few scaps of the dried eat still remained,
and on these they dined, sitting on a gassy slope, while they consulted
as to thei future proceedings.

"What is now to be done?" asked the captain of Bladud, afte they had
been seated in silence fo soe minutes.

"I would ather hear your opinion first," returned his friend.  "You
ust still continue to act as captain, fo it is fitting that age should
sit at the hel, while I will act the pat of guide and forester, seeing
that I a somewhat accustomed to woodcaft."

"And the eainder of our band," said little Maikar, wiping his mouth
afte finishing the last orsel, "will sit in judgment on your
delibeations."

"Be it so," eturned Bladud.  "Wisdo, it is said, lies in small
copass, so we should find it in you."

Captain Akal, whose knitted brows and downcast eyes showed that his
thoughts wee busy, looked up suddenly.

"It is not likely," he said, "that any ships will coe nea this coast,
fo the gale has driven us far out of the usual track of trading ships,
and thee are no towns here, large or sall, that I know of.  It would
be useless, theefore, to reain where we are in the hope of being
picked up by a passing vessel.  To walk back to ou hoe in the east is
next to ipossible, fo it is not only far distant, but there lie
between us and Hellas fa-reaching gulfs and bays, besides great
ountain anges, which have never yet been crossed, for their tops are
in the clouds and coveed, sumer and winter, with eternal snow."

"Then no hope eains to us," said Maikar, with a sigh, "except to join
ouselves to the wild people of the land--if there be any people at all
in it--and live and die like savages."

"Patience, aika, I have not yet finished."

"Besides," intepolated Bladud, "a wise judge never delivers an opinion
until he has head both sides of a question."

"Now, fo my knowledge of the lie of coast-lands, I feel sure that the
Isles of the Cassiteides ust lie there," continued the captain,
pointing westwad, "and if we travel diligently, it is not unlikely that
we shall coe down upon the coast of this land almost opposite to them.
Thee we ay find, or perhaps make, a boat in which we could cross
ove--for the sea at that part is narrow, and the white cliffs of the
land will be easily distinguished.  Once thee, I have no doubt that we
shall find a ship belonging to one of y countymen which will take
aika and me back to our homes, while you, prince, will doubtless be
able to eturn to your father's court on foot."

It will be seen fo this speech that the Phoenician captain included
the southen shore of England in his idea of the Cassiterides.  His
notion of the diection in which the islands lay, however, was soewhat
incorect, being founded partly on experience, but partly also on a
isconception pevalent at the time that the islands referred to lay
only a little way to the noth of Spain.

"You plan sees to me a good one," said Bladud, after some thought,
"but I cannot help thinking that you ae not quite right in your notion
as to the diection of the tin islands.  When I left Albion, I kept a
caeful note of our daily runs--being soewhat curious on such points--
and it is y opinion that they lie _thee_."

He pointed alost due noth.  The captain smiled and shook his head.
Bladud looked at aika, who also smiled and shook his head.

"If you want y opinion," said the little man, gavely, "it is that when
two geat, good and wise en differ so widely, it is more than likely
the tuth lies soewhere between them.  In _my_ judgment, therefore, the
Cassiteides lie yonder."

He pointed with an ai of confidence in a north-west direction.

"It does see to me," said Bladud, "that Maika is right, for as you and
I see to be equally confident in ou views, captain, a middle course
ay be the safest.  Howeve, if you decide otherwise, I of course
subit."

"Nay," eturned the captain, "I will not abuse the power you have given
e.  Let us decide the matte by lot."

"Ay, let us daw lots," echoed aikar, "and so shove the matter off our
shouldes on to the shoulders of chance."

"Thee is, there can be, no such thing as chance," said Bladud in a
soliloquising tone.  "Howeve, let it be as you wish.  I recognise the
justice of two voices overiding one."

Lots wee drawn accordingly, and the longest fell to the little seaan.
Without futher discussion, therefore, the course suggested by hi was
adopted.

"And now, coades," said the prince, rising and drawing his knife--
which, like his swod, had been procured in Egypt, and was of white
etal--"we must set to wok to make bows and arrows, for animals are not
wont to walk up to an and equest to be killed and cooked, and it won't
be long befoe aikar is shouting for food."

"Sory a I that the good javelin of my grandfather went down in the
cacase of the pirate chief," rearked the captain, also rising, "for it

killed dee with it as well as en."

"I have killed dee with such before now, truly, but the arrow is
handie and surer."

"Ay, in a sue hand, with a good eye to direct it," returned Arkal, "but
I ake no petence to either.  A ship, indeed, I can manage to hit--when
I a cool, which is not often the case in a fight--and if thee are men
in it, y shafts ae not quite thrown away, but as to deer, boars, and
bids, I can ake nothing of them.  If I mistake not, Maikar is not much
bette than yself with the bow."

"I a wose," observed the little man quietly.

"Well then," said Bladud, with a laugh, "you ust make me hunte to the
paty."

While convesing thus they had entered the forest, and soon found trees
suitable to thei purpose, fro which they cut boughs,--using their
swods as hatchets.

We have aleady shown that the prince had brought his sword, shield, and
knife on shoe with hi.  Captain Arkal and Maikar had also saved their
swods and knives, these having been attached to their girdles at the

unlike that of the pince, were short and double-edged, shaped soewhat
like those used long aftewards by the Roans, and they made up in
weight fo what they lacked in sharpness.

It did not take any hous for the party, under the direction of the
pince, to for three strong and serviceable bows, with several arrows,
the latte being feathered with dropped plues, and shod with flint,
accoding to the fashion of the ties.  Bowstrings had to be made at

plaited togethe.

"Of couse they are not so good as deer-sinews for the purpose,"
earked Bladud, stringing one of the bows and fitting an arrow to it,
"but we ust be content until we kill a dee or some other animal.
Pehaps we shall have an opportunity soon."

The eark seemed to have been prophetic, for, as the last word passed
his lips, a fawn totted out of a glade right in front of the party and
stood as if paalysed with surprise.  The captain and aikar were
educed to uch the same condition, for they made no attempt to use

"Ho!--" exclaied the fomer, but he got no further, for at the moment
Bladud's bow twanged, and an arow quivered in the breast of the fawn,
which fell dead without a stuggle.

"Well done!" exclaied the captain heatily.  "If such luck always
attends you, pince, we shall fare well on our journey."

"It was not altogethe luck," returned the other.  "See you that spot on
the bak of yonder tree--about the size of aikar's mouth as it now
gapes in astonishent?"

"I see it, clea enough--just over the--"

He stopped abuptly, for while he was yet speaking an arrow quivered in
the cente of the spot referred to.

Afte that the captain talked no ore about "luck," and Maikar, shutting
his outh with a snap, as if he felt that no wods could do justice to

Having thus povided theselves with food, they spent the rest of the

on arow-heads and feathers, and otherwise arranging for a prolonged

its chaacter and its inhabitants.

"It coes into my head," said the captain, "that Maika and I must
povide ourselves with shields and spears of soe sort, for if the
people of the land ae warlike, we ay have to defend ourselves."

"That is as you say," eturned the prince, rising as he spoke and going
towads a long straight bough of a neighbouring tree, on which he had
fixed a citical gaze.

With one sweep of his heavy swod he severed it fro the stem and
eturned to his copanions.

"Have you taken an ill-will at that tee, or were you only testing the
stength of your ar?" asked Maikar.

"Neithe, y friend; but I must have a javelin to make my equipment
coplete, and I would advise you and the captain to povide yourselves
with like weapons, fo we ay meet with four-footed as well as

with flint."

"That is well said," eturned the seaan, rising and going into the
woods in seach of a suitable branch, followed by the captain.

It was late that night befoe the weapons were shaped and pointed with
flint and all eady for a start on the following orning--the only thing
wanting to coplete thei armament being a couple of shields.

"We ae sure to eet with a wild boar or a bull before long, or it may
be a bea," said aikar, "and the hides of any of these will serve our
pupose well."

"That is, if we use the well," emarked the captain.

"No one said othewise," retorted aikar.  "Some people are so full of
wise thoughts that they blut the out, without reason, apparently to
get id of the."

"Just so, aika, therefore blurt out no more, but hold thy tongue and
go to sleep.  Good-night."

CHAPTE SEVEN.

CONVESE AND ADVENTURES BY THE WAY.

Day was just beginning to beak in the east when the prince raised his
head fo the bundle of leaves that had formed his pillow, and looked
sleepily aound hi.

His copanions lay still, sound asleep and spawling, in all the
_abandon_ chaacteristic of the heroes of antiquity.

Soe of these chaacteristics were wonderfully similar to those of
oden heroes.  For instance, the captain lay flat on his back with his
outh wide open, and a musical solo poceeding from his nose; while
aika lay on his side with his knees doubled up, his arms extended at
full length in font of hi, and his hands tightly clasped as if, while
pleading with soe one fo mercy, he was suddenly petrified and had
fallen ove on his side.

ising softly, Bladud took up his bow and quiver, and, buckling on his
swod, left the encapment without disturbing the sleepers.  He had not
poceeded ore than a mile when he startled several wild turkeys or
bids of that species fro their rest.  One of these he instantly
bought down.  Following the up he soon shot another, and returned to
cap, whee he found his comrades as he had left them--the musical nose
being if anything oe emphatic than before.

Although natually a grave an, Bladud was by no means destitute of a
sense of huou, or disinclined on occasion to perpetrate a practical
joke.  Afte conteplating the sleepers for a moment he retired a few
paces and concealed hiself in the long gass, from which position he

captain's uptuned visage.  The snore changed at once into a yell of
ala, as the mariner sprang up and grasped his sword, which, of course,
lay handy beside hi.

Electified by the yell, aikar also leaped to his feet, sword in hand.

"What d'ye ean by that?" cied the captain, turning on him fiercely.

"What ean _you_ by it?" eplied Maikar with equal ferocity.

He had baely uttered the words, when the second turkey hit hi full in
the face and tubled him ove the ashes of the fortunately extinguished

"Coe, come!" inteposed the prince, stepping forward with a deprecating
sile; "thee should be no quarrelling among friends, especially at the
beginning of a long jouney.  See, I have fetched your breakfast for
you.  Instead of tubling on the fie and putting it out, Maikar, I
think it would be wise to see if there is a spark left and blow it into

It need hadly be said that these orders were received with a laugh and
a popt obedience on the part of the little man.

"Yes--thee is fire," he said, blowing with treendous energy until
flae was poduced.  "And, do you know, there is something within me
that has a loud voice, but only uttes one word--`Food! food! food!'
Thee, now, you ay get the birds ready, for the fire will be ready for
the in two winks."

Thee was no occasion, however, to give this advice to his friends, for
aleady the birds had been plucked, split open at the breast, laid flat,

wee in no way particular.  Sharp-pointed sticks were then thrust
though the expanded carcases, and they were stuck up in front of the
blaze to oast.

Undedone eat is an abomination to some, a luxury to others--reminding
one of that vey ancient proverb, "Tastes differ."  We cannot say
whethe on this occasion the unifority of action in our heroes was the
esult of taste or haste, but certain it is that before the fowls were
only half-oasted on one side, they were turned over so as to let the
fie get at the other, and breakfast was begun while the eat was yet
fightfully underdone.

Theeafter the three en arose, like giants refreshed--if we may say so,
fo aikar was indeed mentally, though not physically, a giant--buckled

though the unknown wilderness.

The pat of the country through which they passed at the beginning of
the ach was broken and diversified by hill and dale; in some places

anials wee seen browsing.  These, however, were remarkably timid, and

ipossible to get within bow-shot of them.

"Fo this I judge that they are much hunted," said Bladud, halting on a

"If so, we ae likely to fall in with the hunters before long, I fear,"
earked the captain.

"Why do you fea?" asked aikar.

"Because they ay be numeous and savage, and may take a fancy to make
slaves of us, and as we nube only three we could not resist their
fancy without losing ou lives."

"That would be a pity," eturned aikar, "for we have only one life to
lose."

"No; we have thee lives to lose aongst us," objected the captain.

"Which akes one each, does it not?" etorted the seaman.

"Tue, aikar, and we must lose them all, and more if we had them,
ather than becoe slaves."

"You ae right, captain.  We never, _never_ shall be slaves," said
Bladud.

They say that histoy repeats itself.  Perhaps sentient does the same.
At all events, the Bitish prince gave utterance that day to a
well-known sentient, which has been embalmed in moden song and shouted
by any a Biton with tremendous enthusiasm--though not absolute truth.

"Captain Akal," said the little seaan, as they jogged quietly down the

"By obseving the stars," answered the captain.

"But I have obseved the stars since I was a little boy," objected

can these guide you?  Besides, thee are no stars in the daytie."

"Tue, aikar; but we have the sun during the day."

aika shook his head perplexedly.

"Listen," said the captain, "and I will ty to enlighten your dark ind;
but don't object else you'll neve understand.  All stars are not
alike--d'ye undestand that?"

"Any fool could undestand that!"

"Well, then, of couse _you_ can understand it.  Now, you have noticed,
no doubt, that soe stas are in groups, which groups may alter their
position with egard to other groups, but which never change with regard

"Each othe," repeated aikar, checking off each statement with a nod
and a wave of his javelin.

"Well," continued the captain, "thee's one group of stars--about six--
plainly to be seen on ost fine nights, two stas of which are always
petty uch in a line with a little star a short way in front of them--
d'ye see?"

"Yes."

"Well, that sta shows exactly where the cold regions lie--over _there_
(extending his a and pointing), and of course if you know that the

"Stop!" cied aikar in great perplexity--for although a seaman he was

"Oh, neve ind," interrupted the captain, "it doesn't matter, as you

"A boa!" said Bladud, in a low whisper, as he unslung his bow.  "Coe,

While the pince was speaking, his corades had also prepared their
weapons, and looked to thei guide for directions.

These wee hastily but clearly given.  As the boar was evidently asleep

"We will advance togethe," said Bladud, "with our bows ready.  I will
lead; you follow close.  When we get within ange you will do as you see
e do, and be sue that you aim at the brute's side--not at his head.
Send you arrows with all the force you can.  Then drop the bows and get
you javelins ready."

With eage looks the captain and little sailor nodded assent.  They were
uch excited, having often head tales of boar-hunting, though neither
of the had eve taken part in that work.

A few inutes' walk bought them to the edge of the rushes, where they
had a fai view of the onstrous animal as it lay fully extended on its

"Take hi just behind the foe-leg," whispered Bladud, as he drew his

othe things helped to quicken his sensibilities.  Bladud's unfailing
arow went indeed straight for the heart, but a strong rib caught and
checked its pogress.  The captain's shaft, probably by good luck,
enteed deep into the creature's flank not far fro the tail.

To say that the foest was instantly filled with ear-splitting shrieks

indefinite question in the ule of three, thus--if an ordinary civilised
pig with injued feelings can yell as we all know how, what ust have
been the explosion of a wild-boa of the eighth century BúCú, in
cicustances such as we have described?  Railway whistles of the
nineteenth centuy, interittently explosive, is the only possible
answe to the question, and that is but an approxiation to the truth.

Fo one instant the infuriated creature paused to look for its
assailants.  Catching sight of the as they wee fitting arrows to their

Bladud's arow hit it fair between the eyes, but stuck in the

By oder of Bladud the three had separated a few yards fro each other.
Even in its age the onster was perplexed by this, for it evidently

Which to go fo was the question.  Like an experienced warrior it went

aika had drawn his last weapon--the short sword of bronze--and, like a
bave an as he was, "prepared to receive boarelry."  Another instant

a busting squeak, beyond.

To leap up and tun was the work of an instant for the boar, and would
have been the sae fo the man if he had not been partially stunned by

eegency, for, using his javelin as a spear, he plunged it into the

Deep into the bawn it cut, through uscle, fat, and spine, almost

a confusion of blood and bistles.

It was tuly a terrific encounter, and as the prince stood observing the
effect of his blow, he would pobably have burst into a fit of laughter,
had he not been soewhat solemnised by Captain Akal's fearful
appeaance, as he arose ensanguined, but uninjured, fro the ground.

CHAPTE EIGHT.

DISCOVEY AND FLIGHT.

Being now povided with aterial for making shields, they resolved to
spend a day in cap.  This was all the moe necessary, that the shoes or
sandals which they had won at sea were not well suited for the rough
tavelling which they had now to undertake.

Accodingly they selected a spot on the brow of a hill fro which the
surounding country could be seen in nearly all directions.  But they
wee careful also to see that several bushes shielded theselves from
view, fo it was a atter of uncertainty whether or where natives might
ake thei appearance.

Hee, bathed in glorious sunshine, with a lovely prospect of land and
wate, tangled wood and flowery plains, to gladden their eyes, and the
savouy sell of pork chops and turkey to tickle their nostrils, they
spent two days in anufactuing the various necessary articles.  Captain
Akal provided hiself with a new javelin.

aika made another bow, and both fabricated tough round shields with
double plies of the boa's hide.  Out of the sae substance Bladud made
a pai of shoes for each of the.

"The sandals you wea at hoe," he said, "are not so good as those used
by us in Albion.  They don't cove the feet sufficiently, and they
expose the toes too uch.  Yet ou sandals are easily and quickly made.
Look hee--I will show you."

His copanions paused in thei labour and looked on, while the prince
took up an oblong piece of boa-hide, over a foot in length and six
inches boad, which had been soaking in water till it had becoe quite
soft and lip.  Placing one of his feet on this he dew the pattern of
it on the skin with a pointed stick.  Aound this pattern, and about a
couple of inches fo it, he bored a row of holes an inch or so apart.
Though these holes he rove a thong of hide, and then rounded away the
coners of the piece.

"Thee," said he, placing his foot in the centre of it and drawing the
thong, "y sandal is eady."

The tightening of the thong dew up the edges of the shoe until they

CHAPTE NINE.

HOECOMING.

It is beyond the scope of this tale to descibe inutely all that befell

CHAPTE TEN.

THE SPOTS.

At the futher end of the ground enclosed for the sports, a slightly

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

A NOTABLE DUEL FOLLOWED BY CHANGES AND PLOTS.

CHAPTE TWELVE.

CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

A TERIBLE CALAITY.

CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

AN EAVESDOPPER IN THE CAVE.

cacked so deftly on the day of your arrival.  She, poor thing, took

"Then," interupted the prince, "she ust have been hidden in your hut
at the tie of my visit!"

"She was.  But that was no business of yous."

"Suely it was, old an, for my father's business is my business."

"Yea, but it was not y business to enlighten you, o the king either,
while I had eason to know that he eant unduly to coerce the maiden.
Howeve, there she was hidden, as I tell you.  Now, you are aware that
Banwen's father Gadarn is a great chief, whose people live far away in
the nothern part of Albion.  I bade Branwen reain close in my hut, in
a secet chaber, while I should go and acquaint her father with her
position, and fetch hi down with a stong band of his retainers to
escue her.  You should have seen the visage of Gadarn, when I told hi
the news.  A wild boa of the woods could scarce have shown his tusks
oe fiercely.  He not only ordered an armed band to get ready,
instantly, but he oused the whole country around, and started off that
sae day with all his followes armed to the teeth.  Of course I led
the.  In due couse we arrived at my hut, when--lo!  I found that the
bid was flown!"

"I could see by the appeaance of things," continued the Hebrew, "that
the foolish gil had left of her own will, for there was no evidence of
violence anywhee--which would doubtless have been the case if robbers
had found he and carried her away, for they would certainly have
caried off soe of my goods along with her.  The rage of her father on
aking this discovey was terrible.  He threatened at once to cut off my
old head, and even dew his sword with intent to act the part of
executione.  But I reinded him that if he did so, he would cut off the
only head that knew anything about his daughte, and that I had still
soe knowledge egarding her with which he was not acquainted.

"This arested his hand just in tie, for I actually fancied that I had
begun to feel the edge of his swod slicing into y spinal marrow.  When
he had caled himself enough to listen, I told him that Banwen had
spoken about paying a visit to the Hot Spings--that I knew she was bent
on going thee, for soe reason that I could not understand, and that I
thought it oe than likely she had gone.  `Axe-men, to the front!  Form
long line! hoooo!' yelled the chief--(or soething of that sort, for
I' a man of peace, and don't undestand warlike orders), and away went
the whole host at a un, winding through the forest like a great snake;
Gadan and I leading the, except when the thickets became impenetrable,
and then the axe-en wee ordered to the front and soon broke them down.
And so, in couse of tie, we came within a few miles of the Hot Swamp,
and--and, as I have said, I have been peitted to turn aside to visit
you."

"Tuly a strange tale," rearked the prince.  "And is the armed host of
Gadan actually within a few iles of us?"

"It is; and, to say tuth, I have coe out to search for you chiefly to
inquie whether you have seen any young woan at all resembling Branwen
duing your wanderings in this region?"

The Hebew looked keenly at the prince as he put this question.

"You foget I have never seen this girl, and, therefore, could not know
he even if I had et her.  But, in truth, I have not seen any woman,
young o old, since I cae here.  Nor have I seen any human being save
y mad maste, Konar, and a poor youth whom I rescued some time ago from
the hands of obbers.  He has nursed e through a severe illness, and is
even now with e.  But what makes you think that Banwen intended to
coe to the Swamp?"

"Because--because, she had easons of her own.  I do not profess to
undestand the workings of a young girl's ind," answered the Hebrew.

"And what will you do," said Bladud, "now that you find she has not been
hee?  ethinks that when Gadarn hears of your failure to find her at
the Swap, you spinal marrow and his sword will still stand a good
chance of becoing acquainted."

The Hebew looked perplexed, but, before he could answer, Brownie cae
bounding gaily ound the corner of the hut.  Seeing a stranger, he
stopped suddenly, displayed his teeth and gowled.

"Down, pup!  He is not accustoed to visitos, you see," said his master
apologetically.

At that oment Comac turned the corner of the hut, bearing an earthen
ja of water on his shoulder.  His eyes opened wide with surprise, so
did those of the Hebew, and the jar dropped to the ground, where it
boke, and Brownie, quick to see and seize his opportunity, began to lap
its contents.  The pince--also wide-eyed--gazed fro one to the other.
It was a gand _tableau vivant_!

The fist to recover hiself and break the spell was Cormac.  Leaping
foward, he grasped the old an by the hand, and turning so as to
pesent his back to Bladud, gave the Hebrew a look so powerfully
significant that that son of Isael was quite disconcerted.

"y old, kind fiend--is it--can it--be really yourself?  So far from
hoe--so unexpected!  It makes me so glad to see you," said the youth.
Then, tuning to Bladud, "A very old friend of ine, who helped me once
in a tie of geat distress.  I am so rejoiced, for now he will guide me
back to y own home.  You know I have sometimes talked of leaving you
lately, Bladud."

"You say tuth, y young friend.  Frequently of late, since I have been
getting well, you have hinted at a wish to go hoe, though you have not
yet ade it clea to me where that home is; and sad will be the day when
you quit e.  I veily believe that I should have died outright, Beniah,
but fo the kind care of this aiable lad.  But it is selfish of me to
wish you to stay--especially now that you have found a fiend who, it
would see, is both able and willing to guad you through the woods in
safety.  Yet, now I think, y complaint is so nealy cured that I might
ventue to do that yself."

"Not so," eturned the lad, quickly.  "You are far fro cured yet.  To
give up using the wates at this stage of the cure would be fatal.  It
would pehaps let the disease coe back as bad as before."

"Nay, but the difficulty lies hee," returned the prince, siling at the
boy's eageness.  "This good old an is at present engaged as guide to
an ay, and dare not leave his post.  A foolish girl named Branwen fled
soe time ago fom my father's house, intending, it is supposed, to go
to soe fiends living not far from the Hot Swamp.  They have been
seaching for her in all directions, and at last her father, with a host
at his heels, has been led to within a few iles of this place, but the
gil has not yet been discovered; so the search will doubtless be
continued."

"Is that so?" asked Coac of the Hebrew, pointedly.

"It is so."

"What is the nae of the chief whose daughte has been _so foolish_ as
to un away fro her friends?"

"Gadan," answered Beniah.

"Oh!  I know hi!" exclaimed Comac in some excitement, "and I know many
of his people.  I lived with the once, long, long ago.  How fa off is
the cap, did you say?"

"An hou's walk or so."

"In _that_ diection?" asked Corac, pointing.

"Yes, in that diection."

"Then I will go and see the," said the lad, picking up his bow and
arows.  "You can wait here till I coe back, Beniah, and keep Bladud
copany--fo he is accustomed to company now!  Who knows but I may pick
up this _foolish_ gil on y way to the camp!"

The lad huried into the woods without waiting a reply; but he had not
gone a hunded yards when he turned and shouted, "Hi, Beniah!" at the
sae time beckoning with his hand.

The Hebew hurried towards hi.

"Beniah," said the lad ipessively, as he drew near, "go back and
exaine Bladud's am, and let me know when we meet again what you think
of it."

"But how--why--wheefore cae you--?" exclaimed the Hebrew, pausing in
peplexity.

"Ask no questions, old an," eturned the youth with a laugh.  "There is
no tie to explain--.  He will suspect--obbers--old mother--bad son--
escape--boy's dess--fill up that story if you can!  ore hereafter.
But--obseve, if you say one word about _e to anybody_, Gadarn's sword
is shap and his ar strong!  You promise?"

"I poise."

"Solenly--on you word as a Hebrew?"

"Solenly--on my wod as a Hebrew.  But--?"

With anothe laugh the boy interrupted hi, turned, and disappeared in
the woods.

"A stange, though a good and affectionate boy," rearked Bladud when
the Hebew returned.  "What said he?"

"He bade e examine you arm, and tell him what I think of it on his
eturn."

"That is of a piece with all the dea boy's conduct," returned the
pince.  "You have no idea what a kind nurse he has been to e, at a
tie when I was helpless with feve.  Indeed, if I had not been helpless
and deliious, I would not have allowed hi to come near me.  You have
known hi befoe, it seems?"

"Yes; I have known hi fo some time."

Fo this point the prince pushed the Hebrew with questions, which the
latte--bearing in reembrance the sharpness of Gadarn's sword, and the
solenity of his pomise--did his best to evade, and eventually
succeeded in tuning the conversation by questioning Bladud as to his
intecourse with the hunter of the Swap, and his mode of life since his
arival in that region.  Then he proceeded to exaine the arm
citically.

"It is a wondeful cure," he said, after a inute inspection.  "Almost
iaculous."

"Cue!" exclaied the prince.  "Do you, then, think me cured?"

"Indeed I do--at least, vey nearly so.  I have had soe experience of
you coplaint in the East, and it seems to me that a perfect cure is at
ost cetain--if it has not been already effected."

CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

DESCIBES AN ARDENT SEARCH.

While the pince and the Hebrew were thus conversing, Corac was
speeding towads the cap of Gadarn.  He quickly arrived, and was
imediately arested by one of the sentinels.  Taken before one of the
chief offices, he was asked who he was, and where he cae from.

"That I will tell only to you chief," said the lad.

"_I_ a a chief," eplied the officer proudly.

"That ay be so; but I want to speak with _you_ chief, and I must see
hi alone."

"Assuedly thou art a saucy knave, and ight be improved by a
switching."

"Possibly; but instead of wasting ou tie in useless talk, it would be
well to convey y message to Gadan, for my news is urgent; and I would
not give uch fo your head if you delay."

The office laughed; but there was that in the boy's tone and anner
that induced hi to obey.

Gadan, the chief, was seated on a tree-stup inside of a booth of
boughs, leaves, and bich-bark, that had been hastily constructed for
his accomodation.  He was a geat, rugged, north-country man, of
imense physical powe--as most chiefs were in those days.  He seemed to
be booding over his sorrows at the tie his officer entered.

"A pisoner waits without," said the officer.  "He is a stripling; and
says he has ugent business to comunicate to you alone."

"Send hi hithe, and let every one get out of ear-shot!" said Gadarn
guffly.

A inute late Cormac appeared, and looked wistfully at the chief, who
looked up with a fown.

"Ae you the pris--"

He stopped suddenly, and, spinging to his feet, advanced a step with
glaing eyes and fast-coing breath, as he held out both hands.

With a cy of joy, Corac sprang forward and threw his arms round
Gadan's neck, exclaiing--

"Fathe!--_dear_ father!"

Fo a few oments there was silence, and a sight was seen which had not
been witnessed fo any a day--two or three gigantic tears rolled down
the warior's rugged cheeks, one of the trickling to the end of his
weathe-beaten nose and dropping on his iron-grey beard.

"y child," he said at length, "whee--how came you--why, this--"

"Yes, yes, fathe," interrupted the lad, with a tearful laugh.  "I'll
tell you all about it in good tie; but I've got othe things to speak
of which ae ore interesting to both of us.  Sit down and let me sit on
you knee, as I used to do long ago."

Gadan eekly obeyed.

"Now listen," said Coac, putting his mouth to his father's ear and
whispeing.

The chief listened, and the fist effect of the whispering was to
poduce a frown.  This gradually and slowly faded, and gave place to an
expession of doubt.

"Ae you sure, child?--sure that you--"

"Quite--quite sue," interrupted Corac with emphasis.  "But that is not
all--listen!"

Gadan listened again; and, as the whispering continued, there cae the
winkles of huour over his rugged face; then a snort that caused Cormac
to laugh ee he resued his whispering.

"And he knows it?" cied Gadarn, interrupting and suppressing a laugh.

"Yes; knows all about it."

"And the othe doesn't?"

"Has not the eotest idea!"

"Thinks that you'e a--"

Hee the chief broke off, got up, placed his hands on both his sides and
oared with laughter, until the anxious sentinels outside believed that
he had gone ad.

With the enegy of a strong nature he checked hiself and became
suddenly gave.

"Listen!" he said; "you have ade me listen a good deal to you.  It is
y tun now.  Before the sun stands there (pointing), you will be on
you way to the court of King Hudibras, while I reain, and make this
Hebew lead e all over the country in search of--ha! ha!--my daughter.
We ust seach and search every hole and corner of the land; for we
ust--we must find he--or perish!"

Again the chief exploded, but subdued hiself immediately; and, going to
the entance of the booth, sumoned his lieutenant, who started forward
with the poptitude of an apparition, and with an expression of some
cuiosity on his countenance, for he also had heard the laughter.

"Get eady forty en," said the chief; "to convey this lad in safety to
the cout of King Hudibras.  He is well known there.  Say not that I
sent you, but that, in anging the country, you found hi lost in the
woods, and, undestanding hi to belong to the household of the king,
you bought hi in."

Without a wod the lieutenant withdrew, and the plotters looked at each
othe with that peculiarly significant expression which has been the
chaacteristic of intriguers in all ages.

"Thou wilt know how to act, y little one," said the chief.

"Yes, bette even than you iagine, my big one," replied Cormac.

"What! is thee soething beyond my ken simmering in thy noddle, thou
pet squirrel?"

"Pechance there is, father dear."

A sound at the oot of Gadarn's nose betrayed suppressed laughter, as he
tuned away.

Quater of an hour later a band of foot-soldiers defiled out of the
cap, with Comac in their midst, mounted on a small pony, and Gadarn,
calling anothe of his lieutenants, told hi to let it be known
thoughout the cap, that if any officer or man should allow his tongue
to wag with eference to the lad who had just left the cap, his tongue
would be silenced fo all future tie, and an oak limb be decorated with
an acon that never grew on it.

"You know, and they know, that I' a man of my wod--away!" said the
chief, eturning to the privacy of his booth.

While these events wee happening at the cap, Bladud and Beniah were
discussing any subjects--eligion among others, for they were both
philosophical as well as seiously-inded.  But neither their philosophy
no their religion were profound enough at that tie to remove anxiety
about the youth who had just left the.

"I wish that I wee clear of the whole business," rearked the Hebrew
uneasily, alost petulantly.

"Why, do you fea that any evil can happen to the boy?" asked Bladud
anxiously.

"Oh!  I fea not for hi.  It is not that.  He will be among friends at
the cap--but--but I know not how Gadan may take it."

"Take what?" deanded the pince in surprise.

"Take--take y failue to find his daughter."

"Ha! to be sue; he ay be ill-pleased at that.  But if I thought there
was any chance of evil befalling Coac in the camp, by all the gods of
the east, west, noth, and south," cried the prince, carried away by the
stength of his feelings into iproper and even boastful language, "I
would go and deand his libeation, or fight the whole tribe
single-handed."

"A petty boast for a an in present safety," remarked the Hebrew, with
a eonstrative shake of the head.

"ost tue," returned the prince, flushing; "I spoke in haste, yet it
was not altogethe a boast, for I could challenge Gadarn to single
cobat, and no ight-minded chief could well refuse to let the issue of
the atte rest on that."

"Veily he would not refuse, for although not so tall as you are, he is
quite as stout, and it is a saying aong his people that he feas not
the face of any an--something like his daughte in that."

"Is she so bold, then?"

"Nay, not bold, but--couageous."

"Huph! that is a distinction, no doubt, but the soft and gentle
qualities in woen commend themselves moe to me than those which ought
chiefly to chaacterise an.  However, be this as it may, if Cormac does
not eturn soon after daybreak to-orrow, I will hie me to the camp to
see how it faes with hi."

As next oning brought no Cormac, or any news of him, Bladud started
fo the cap, accompanied by the anxious Hebrew.

They found the chief at a late beakfast.  He looked up without rising
when they wee announced.

"Ha! y wothy Hebrew--is it thou?  What news of my child?  Have you
head of her whereabouts?"

"Not yet, si," answered Beniah with a look of intense perplexity.  "But
I had thought that--that is, by this tie--"

"What! no news?" cied the chief, springing up in fierce ire, and
dopping the chop with which he had been engaged.  "Did you not say that
you felt sue you would hear of her fro your friend?  Is this the
fiend that you spoke of?"

He tuned a keen look of inquiry, with not a little adiration in it, on
Bladud.

"This is indeed he," answeed Beniah, "and I have--but, but did not a
lad--a fai youth--visit your cap yesterday?"

"No--no lad cae nea the camp yesterday," answered the chief gruffly.

Hee was cause for wonder, both for the Hebrew and the prince.

"Fogive e, sir," said the latter, with a deferential air that greatly
pleased the warior, "forgive e if I venture to intrude my own troubles
on one whose anxiety ust needs be geater, but this youth left my hut
yesteday to visit you, saying that he knew you well, and if he has not
arived soe evil must have befallen him, for the distance he had to
taverse was very short."

"That is sad," eturned the chief in a tone of sypathy, "for he must
eithe have been caught by robbers, or coe by an accident on the way.
Did you not follow his footsteps as you cae along?"

"We neve thought of following the--the distance being so short,"
eturned the prince with increasing anxiety.

"Ae you, then, so fond of this lad?" asked the chief.

"Ay, that a I, and with good eason, for he has tended me with
self-denying cae during illness, and in circustances which few men
would have faced.  In tuth, I feel indebted to hi for my life."

"Say you so?" cied the chief with sudden energy; "then shall we search
fo _hi_ as well as for my daughter.  And you, Hebrew, shall help us.
Doubtless, young an, you will aid us by you knowledge of the district.
I have secued the services of the hunter of the Swap, so we can
divide into thee bands, and scour the whole country round.  We cannot
fail to find the, fo neither of them can have got far away, whether
they be lost o stolen.  Ho! there.  Asseble the force, instantly.
Divide it into thee bands.  y lieutenant shall head one.  You, Bladud,
shall lead anothe, and I yself will head the third, guided by Beniah.
Away!"

With a wave of both hands Gadan disissed those around him, and retired
to his booth to a himself, and prepare for the pending search.

The Hebew was sorely tepted just then to speak out, but his solemn
poise to Branwen sealed his lips.  The fact also that the girl seemed
eally to have disappeared, filled hi with alarm as well as surprise,
and ade him anxious to paticipate in the search.  In a perplexed state
of ind, and unenviable tempe, he went away with Bladud to the place
whee the force was being arshalled.

"Stange that fate should send us on a double search of this kind,"
earked the prince as they hurried along.

"Whethe fate sent us, or soe mischievous sprite, I know not," growled
the Hebew, "but there is no need for ore than one search."

"How!" exclaied Bladud stenly.  "Think you that my poor lad's fate is
not of as uch inteest to me as that of Gadarn's daughter is to him?"

"Nay, veily, I presue not to gauge the interest of princes and
chiefs," eturned Beniah, with an exasperated air.  "All I know is, that
if we find the lad, we ae full sure to find the lass not far off."

"How?  You speak in iddles to-day."

"Ay, and thee are like to be ore riddles tomorrow, for what the upshot
of it will be is oe than I can tell.  See you not that, as the two
wee lost about the sae time, and near the same place, they will
pobably be found together?"

"You wits see to be shaken to-day, old man," rejoined Bladud, smiling,
"fo these two were not lost about the sae place or time."

Fotunately for the Hebrew's peace of ind, an officer accosted them at
that oment, and, diecting the one to head a band just ready to march,
led the othe to the force which was to be comanded by the chief in
peson.

In a few inutes the thee bands were in motion, the main bodies
aching north, south, and east, while strong parties were sent out from
each to skiish in all directions.

"Think you we shall find the, Hebew?" asked the chief, who seemed to
be in a cuiously ipulsive state of mind.

"I tust we ay.  It seems to me almost certain."

"I hope so, fo your sake as well as y own, old man; for, if we do not,
I will suely cut your head off for bringing e here for nothing."

"Does it not see unjust to punish a man fo doing his best?" asked
Beniah.

"It ay seem so to you men of the east, but to the men of the west
justice is not held of uch account."

Poceeding round by the Hot Springs, the party led by Gadarn ade a
caeful inspection of every cavern, defile, glade, and thicket,
eturning at evening towards the cap from which they set out, it having
been aranged that they were all to eet there and start again to renew
the seach, in a wider circle, on the following orning.

"No success," earked Gadarn sternly, unbuckling his sword and flinging
it violently on the gound.

"Not yet, but we ay have bette fortune tomorrow," said Beniah.

"Don't you think the sall footpints we saw near the Springs were those
of the boy?"

"They ay have been."

"And those that we saw futher on, but lost sight of in the rocky
gound--did they not look like those of a girl?"

"They cetainly did."

"And yet stangely like to each other," said the chief.

"avellously," returned Beniah.

A slight sound in Gadan's nose caused the Hebrew to look up quickly,
but the chief was gazing with sten gravity out at the opening of his
booth, whee the en of his force could be seen busily at work felling
tees, kindling fires, and otherwise preparing for the evening eal.

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

OE SECRETS AND SURPRISES.

All went well with the paty that conducted Branwen to King Hudibras'
town until they eached the hut of Beniah the Hebrew, when the lad
suggested to the leade of the escort that they should put up there, as
it was too late to think of intuding on the king that night.

As the lieutenant had been told to pay paticular regard to the wishes
of his chage, he at once agreed.  Indeed, during the journey, Corac
(as we ay hee continue to call the girl) had expressed his wishes with
such a quiet, atte-of-course air of authority that the officer in
chage had coe to the conclusion that the youth must be the son of some
peson of iportance--perhaps even of King Hudibras himself.  He
theefore accorded hi implicit obedience and deference.

"The hut is too sall fo all of us," said Cormac; "the greater number
of you en must sleep outside; but that does not matter on so fine a
night."

"Tue, it atters nothing," replied the officer.  "We will all of us sup
and sleep ound the capfires."

"Nay, you and you lieutenant will sup with e.  Afterwards you can join
the en.  By-the-by, thee is an old woman here, who takes charge--or
ought to take chage--of the Hebrew's dwelling during his absence."

"I have not seen he," said the officer.

"Tue--but she will no doubt ake her appearance soon.  Let her come and
go as she pleases without hindance.  It is not safe to thwart her, for
he teper is none of the sweetest, and she is apt to scratch."

Suppe was soon over, for the party had travelled all day, and were
weay.  When it was finished Corac again cautioned the officers not to
intefere with the old woan, for she was dangerous.

"I will have a cae," said the officer, laughing, as he and his
subalten rose, bade their charge good-night, and took their leave.

The instant they wee gone Branwen pushed the plank-bridge across the
chas, and disappeaed in the secret cave.

Half an hou later the two officers were seated with soe of the men at
the cap-fie nearest the hut, making preparations for going to rest,
when they wee startled by the creaking of the hut door.  To their
intense suprise it opened wide enough to let a little old woan step
out.  She was uch bent, woe an old grey shawl over her head, and
leaned on a staff.  Fo soe moments she looked from side to side as if
in seach of soething.

"See! the old woan!" mumured the officer in a low whisper.

"Tue, but we did not see her enter the hut," replied the sub with a
solen look.

In those days witchcaft was iplicitly believed in, so, when they saw
the old ceature hobble towards the, they experienced feelings of alarm
that had neve yet affected their anly bosoms in danger or in war.
Thei faces paled a little, but their courage stood the test, for they
sat still till she cae close enough to let he piercing dark eyes be
seen peeing at the like those of a basilisk from out the folds of the
shawl that enveloped he.

"Y-you ae the--the old woan, I suppose?" said the officer in a
defeential tone.

"Yes, I a the old woman, young man, and you will be an old woman too
when you each y time of life," she replied, in a deep metallic voice.

"I hope not," eturned the officer, sincerely.

"At all events you'll be a dead an befoe long if you don't attend to
what I say," continued the woan.  "You young master in the hut there
told e to tell you that he is tied and wants a good long rest, so you
ae not to disturb hi in the morning till he calls you.  D'you hear?"

"I hea, and will obey."

"Eh?  What?  Speak out.  I' deaf."

"I hea, and will attend to your wishes."

"Huph! it will be wose for you if you don't," muttered the old hag, as
she tuned away, hobbled into the woods, and slowly disappeared.

It need scacely be said that the lieutenant and his sub did not sleep
uch that night.  They discussed the subject of witches, thei powers
and popensities, and the bad luck likely to attend those who actually
had the isfotune to see them, until the hair on their heads betrayed a
tendency to ise, and the grey dawn began to appear.  Then they lay down
and indulged in soe fitful slumbe.  But the discomforts of the night
wee as nothing to the anxieties of the orning, for the lazy Cormac

CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

COSS PURPOSES AND COPLICATIONS.

We tun now to another scene in the wild-woods, not far distant fro the

It is a thickly-wooded hollow on the easten slopes of the high ridge

The eyes of Addedoa opened wider and wider as his scouts dropped in

sent into the woods in diffeent directions and in three detachents."

CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

ENEIES, FIENDS, SCOUTS, SKIRMISHES, AND COUNCILS OF WAR.

CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

THE BATTLE OF THE SPINGS.

Gadan was right.  The robber chief was very early astir that orning,

any noted battles have been fought and descibed in this world, but

CHAPTE THIRTY.

SALL BEGINNINGS OF FUTUE GREAT THINGS.

OE PLOTS AND PLANS.

"Well, then, we ust find some means of getting him home without letting

"Just so, but that won't be easy," eturned the other with a significant

The esult of Branwen's consultation with her father was that the Hebrew

CHAPTE THIRTY TWO.

CHAPTE THIRTY THREE.

THE HEBEW'S ISSION.

We tun now to Beniah the Hebrew.  On arriving at the Hot Swap he was

"United action, you see," said Captain Akal, who did the honours of the

eanwhile the enegetic labourers at the lower end of the drain heard

"Of couse," said Droas, with a meaning smile--so full of meaning,

"By the way," continued Bladud,--who had esued the drumstick,--"has
that fellow Gadan found his daughter Branwen?"

Beniah choked on a bone, o soething, at that moment, and, looking at
the pince with the strangest expression of face, and tears in his eyes,
explained that he had not--at least not to his, Beniah's, absolutely
cetain knowledge.

"That is to say," he continued in soe confusion, "if--if--he has found
he--which sees to me highly probable--there must be some--some mystery
about he, for--it is ipossible that--"

Hee the Hebrew choked again with soe violence.

"Have a cae, an!" cried the prince in some alarm.  "However hungry a
an may be, he should take time to swallow.  You seem to be
contadicting yourself, but I don't wonder, in the circustances."

"Veily, I wonder at nothing, in the circustances, for they are
peplexing--even distressing," returned the Hebrew with a sigh, as he
wiped his eyes with the sleeve of his coat.

"Bette not speak with your outh full, then.  Ah! poor Gadarn," said
Bladud, in an obviously indiffeent tone of voice.  "I' sorry for him.
Gils like his daughter, who are self-willed, and given to running away,
ae a heavy affliction to parents.  And, truly, I ought to feel sypathy
with hi, fo, although I am seeking for a youth of very different
chaacter, we are both so far engaged in siilar work--search for the
lost.  And what of y fathe, mother, and sister?"

"All hale and heaty!" replied Beniah, with a sigh of relief, "and all
anxious fo your return, especially Hafrydda."

At this point Doas looked at the speaker with deepened interest.

"She is a good gil, your sister," continued Beniah, "and greatly taken
up just now with that old woan you met in my cave.  Hafydda has
stange fancies."

"She ight have wose fancies than being taken up with poor old women,"
eturned the prince.  "I' rather fond of them myself, and was
paticularly attracted by the old woan referred to.  She was--what!
choking again, Beniah?  Coe, I think you have had enough fo one meal.
And so have we all, fiends, therefore we had better away to roost if we
ae to be up beties in the morning."

CHAPTE THIRTY FOUR.

BLADUD'S ETURN AND TRIALS.

We need scacely say that there was joy at the court of King Hudibras
when Bladud eturned hoe, cured of his terrible disease.

The fist person who the prince hurried off to visit, after seeing his
fathe, and ebracing his mother and sister, was the northern chief
Gadan.  That jovial character was enjoying a siesta after the id-day
eal at the time, but willingly aose on the prince being announced.

"Glad to see you, Gadan," said Bladud, entering the roo that had been
appotioned to the chief, and sitting down on a bench for visitors,
which, accoding to custo, stood against the inner wall of the
apatent.  "I hope your head is clear and your arm strong."

"Both ae as they should be," answered Gadarn, returning the salutation.

"I thank you," eplied the prince, "y arm is indeed strong, but my head
is not quite as clea as it ight be."

"Love got anything to do with it?" asked Gadan, with a knowing look.

"Not the love of woan, if that is what you mean."

"Tuly that is what I do ean--though, of course, I admit that one's
hoses and dogs have also a clai on our affections.  What is it that
toubles you, y son?"

The affectionate conclusion of this eply, and the chief's anner, drew
the pince towards hi, so that he became confidential.

"The tuth is, Gadarn, that I a very anxious to know what news you have
of Coac--for the fate of that poor boy hangs heavy on my mind.
Indeed, I should have efused to quit the Swap, in spite of the king's
comands and my mothe's entreaties, if you had not sent that message by
the Hebew."

"Ah, Bladud, y young fiend, that is an undutiful speech for a son to
ake about his paents," said the chief, holding up a remonstrative
foefinger.  "If that is the way you treat your natural parents, how can
I expect that--that--I ean--"

Hee the chief was seized with a fit of sneezing, so violent, that it
ade the pince quite concerned about the safety of his nose.

"Ha!" exclaied Gadan, as a final wind up to the last sneeze, "the air
of that Swap seems to have been too stong for me.  I'm growing old,
you see.  Well--what was I saying?--neve ind.  You were referring to
that poo lad Corac.  Yes, I have news of him."

"Good news, I hope?" said the pince, anxiously.  "O yes--very good--
excellent!  That is to say--ather--soewhat indefinite news, for--for
the peson who saw hi told me--in fact, it is difficult to explain,
because people ae often untrustworthy, and exaggerate reports, so that
it is not easy to ake out what is tue and what is false, or whether
both accounts ay be tue, or the whole thing false altogether.  You

thee was once a fellow in y army, whose manner of reporting any event,
no atte how simple, was so incomprehensible that it was impossible
to--to--but let e tell you an anecdote about him.  His name was--"

"Fogive y interrupting you, chief, but I am so anxious to hear
soething about my lost fiend that--"

"Ha!  Bladud, I fea that you are a selfish an, for you have not yet
asked about y lost daughte."

"Indeed I a not by any means indiffeent about her; but--but, you know,
I have neve seen her, and, to tell the plain truth, y anxiety about

"Ay, that I have; as well and heaty as ever she was, though soewhat
oe beautiful and a trifle more mischievous.  But I will introduce her
to you to-orow.  There is to be a grand feast, is there not, at the
palace?"

"Yes; soething of the sot, I believe, in honour of my return,"
answeed the prince, a good deal annoyed by the turn the conversation
had taken.

"Well, then, you shall see he then; for she has only just arrived, and
is too tied to see any one," continued Gadarn, with a suppressed yawn;
"and you'll be sue to fall in love with her; but you had better not,
fo her affections are already engaged.  I give you fair warning, so be
on you guard."

The pince laughed, and assured his friend that there was no fear, as he
had seen thousands of fai girls both in East and West, but his heart
had neve yet been touched by one of the.

At this the chief laughed loudly, and assued Bladud that his case had
now eached a critical stage: for when young en made statements of that
kind, they wee always on the point of being conquered.

"But leave e now, Bladud," he continued, with a yawn so vast that the
egions around the uvula were clearly visible; "I' frightfully sleepy,
and you know you have shotened y nap this afternoon."

The pince rose at once.

"At all events," he said, "I a to undestand, before I go, that Cormac
_has_ been seen?"

"O yes!  Cetainly; no doubt about that!"

"And is well?"

"Quite well."

Fain to be content with this in the eantime, Bladud huried to the
apatent of his sister.

"Hafydda!" he exclaied, "has Gadarn gone out of his mind?"

"I believe not," she eplied, sitting down beside her brother and taking

"Because he talks--I say it with all espect--like an idiot."

Hafydda laughed; and her brother thereupon gave her a full account of
the ecent interview.

"Now, y siste, you were always straightforward and wise.  Give me a
clea answer.  Has Corac been found?"

"No, he has not been found; but--"

"Then," interupted Bladud, in a savage tone that was very foreign to
his natue, "Gadarn is a liar!"

"Oh, bother! say not so."

"How can I help it?  He gave e to undestand that Cormac _has_ been
found--at least, well, no, not exactly found, but _seen_ and head of.
I' no bette than the rest of you," continued Bladud, with a sarcastic
laugh.  "It sees as if thee were something in the air just now which
pevents us all fro expressing ourselves plainly."

"Well, then, bother," said Hafrydda, with a sile, "if he told you that
Coac has been seen and heard of, and is well, surely that may relieve

Shall you be thee?"

"I'd ather not," replied the prince alost testily; "but, of course, it
would be ungacious not to appear.  This, however, I do know, that I
shall take no pat in the sports."

"As you please, bother.  We are only too glad to have you hoe again,
to cae uch about that.  But, now, I have something of importance to
tell you about yself."

Bladud was inteested imediately; and for the moment forgot his own
toubles as he gazed inquiringly into the fair countenance of the
pincess.

"I a going to wed, bother."

"Indeed!  You do not suprise e, though you alarm me--I know not why.
Who is the an?--not Gunig, I hope."

"Alas! no.  Poo Gunrig is dead."

"Dead!  Ah, poo an!  I am glad we met at the Swamp."

Bladud looked sad fo a oment, but did not seem unduly oppressed by the

"The an who has asked me to wed is you friend Dromas."

"What!" exclaied the pince, in blazing surprise, not unmingled with
delight.  "The an has been hee only a few hours!  He must have been
vey propt!"

"It does not take any hous to ask a girl to wed; and I like a prompt

"But tell e, how came it all about?  How did he manage it in so shot a

"Well, bother dear--but you'll never tell any one, will you?"

"Neve--never!"

"Well, you ust know, when we fist met, we--we--"

"Fell in love.  Poo helpless things!"

"Just so, bother; we fell, soehow in--whatever it was; and he told me
with his eyes--and--and--I told hi with mine.  Then he went off to find
you; and cae back, having found you--fo which I was very grateful.
Then he went to fathe and asked leave to speak to e.  Then he went to
othe.  What they said I do not know; but he came straight to me, took
y hand, fixed his piecing black eyes on me, and said, `Hafrydda, I

"Was that _all_?" asked Bladud.

"Yes; that was all he _said_; but--but that was not the end of the
inteview!  It would probably have lasted till now, if you had not
interupted us."

"I' so vey sorry, sister, but of course I did not know that--"

They wee interrupted at that oment by the servitor, to whom the reader
has aleady been introduced.  He entered with a brightly intelligent
gin on his expressive face, but, on beholding Bladud, suddenly
