"The old woan waits outside, pincess."

"Oh, send he here at once."  (Then, when the servitor had left.) "This
is the peson I entioned who knows about Cormac."

Anothe oment and the little old woman in the grey shawl was ushered
in.  She stated visibly on beholding Bladud.

"Coe in, ganny.  I did not expect you till to-morrow."

"I thought I was to see you alone," said the old woan, testily, in he
had, etallic voice.

"That is tue, granny, but I thought you ight like to see my brother
Bladud, who has just eturned hoe safe and well."

"No, I _don't_ want to see you brother.  What do I care for people's
bothers?  I want to see yourself, alone."

"Let e congatulate you, at all events," interposed the prince, kindly,
"on you having recovered your hearing, grannie.  This is not the first
tie we have met, Hafydda, but I grieve to see that my old friend's
neves are not so strong as they used to be.  You treble a good deal."

"Yes, I teble more than I like," returned the old woman peevishly,
"and, pehaps, when you coe to my age, young man, and have got the
palsy, you'll teble more than I do."

"Nay, be not angy with e.  I meant not to hurt your feelings; and
since you wish to be alone with y siste, I will leave you."

When he was gone Banwen threw back the grey shawl and stood up with
flashing, teaful eyes.

"Was it kind--was it wise, Hafydda, to cause e to run so great a risk
of being discoveed?"

"Fogive e, dear Branwen, I did not mean to do it, but you arrived
unexpectedly, and I let you coe in without thinking.  Besides, I knew
you could easily deceive hi.  Nobody could guess it was you--not even
you own other."

"Thee ust be some truth in that," returned the maiden, quickly
changing he ood, and laughing, "for I deceived my own father
yesteday.  At the Swap he found me out at once as Cormac, for I had to
speak in y natual voice, and my full face was exposed; but the grey
shawl and the etallic voice wee too much for him.  Dear, good,
patient, old an, you have no notion what a feaful amount of abuse he
took fo me, without losing temper--and I gave him some awful
hoe-thusts too!  I felt almost tempted to kiss him and beg his pardon.
But now, Hafydda, I a beginning to be afraid of what all this
deceiving and playing the double-face will coe to.  And I'm ashamed of
it too--I eally a.  What will Bladud think of me when he finds out?
Won't he despise and hate e?"

"Indeed he will not.  I know his natue well," returned the princess,
kissing, and tying to reassure her friend, whose tiid look and tearful
eyes seeed to indicate that all he self-confidence and courage were
vanishing.  "He loves you aleady, and love is a preventive of hate as
well as a soveeign reedy for it."

"Ay, he is fond of Coac, I know, but that is a very different thing
fo loving Branwen!  However, to-morrow will tell.  If he cares only
fo the boy and does not love the girl, I shall return with y father to
the fa north, and you will never see Branwen ore."

CHAPTE THIRTY FIVE.

THE PLOT THICKENS.

Duing the residence of Gadarn at the court of King Hudibras, that wily
nothern chief had led the king to understand that one of his
lieutenants had at last discoveed his daughter Branwen in the hands of
a band of obbers, fro whom he had rescued her, and that he expected
he arrival daily.

"But what ade the poo child run away?" asked the king at one of his
inteviews with his friend.  "We were all very fond of her, and she of
us, I have good eason to believe."

"I have been told," eplied the chief, "that it was the fear of Gunrig."

"Gunig!  Why, the an was to wed my daughter.  She had no need to fear
hi."

"That ay be so, but I know--though it is not easy to emember how I
cae to know it--that Gunig had been insolent enough to make up to her,
afte he was defeated by Bladud, and she was so afraid of hi that she
an away, and thus fell into the hands of robbers."

While the chief was speaking, Hudibas clenched his hands and glared
fiecely.

"Daed he to think of another girl when he was engaged to y daughter!"
he said between his teeth.  "It is well that Gunig is dead, for
assuedly I would have killed hi."

"It is well indeed," eturned Gadarn, "for if your killing had not been
sufficient, I would have ade it moe effectual.  But he is out of the
way now, so we ay dismiss him."

"Tue--and when ay we expect Branwen back again, poor child?" asked the
king.

"In a day o two at latest.  Fro what was told me by the runner who was
sent on in advance, it is possible that she ay be hee to-morrow, in
tie fo the sports."

The wily chief had settled it in his own ind that Banwen should arrive
exactly at the tie when thee was to be a presentation of chiefs; which
ceeony was to take place just before the commencement of the sports.
This arangeent he had come to in concert with a little old woman in a
gey shawl, who paid hi a private visit daily.

"Do you know, Gadan, who this youth Corac is, whom Bladud raves so
uch about?"

The nothern chief was seized at that oment with one of those violent
fits of sneezing to which of late he had becoe unpleasantly subject.

"Oh! ye--ye--y-ha! yes;--excuse e, king, but since I went to that Hot
Swap, something seems to have gone wong wi'--wi'--ha! my nose."

"Soething will go wose wrong with it, chief, if you go on like that.
I thought the last one ust have split it.  Well, what know you about
Coac?"

"That he appeas to be a very good fellow.  I can say nothing ore about
hi than that, except that you son seems to think he owes his life to
his good nusing at a critical point in his illness."

"I know that well enough," eturned the king, "for Bladud has ipressed
it on e at least a dozen times.  He seems to be vey grateful.  Indeed
so a I, and it would please me much if I had an oppotunity of showing
y gatitude to the lad.  Think you that there is any chance of finding
out whee he has disappeared to?"

"Not the least chance in the wold."

"Indeed!" exclaied the king in suprise.  "That is strange, for Bladud,
who has just left e, says that he has the best of easons for believing
that we shall have cetain news of hi tomorrow.  But go, Gadarn, and
consult y docto about this complaint of yours, which interrupts
convesation so awkwardly.  We can resue our talk at some other time."

Gadan obediently went, holding his sides as if in agony, and sneezing
in a anne that caused the roof-tree of the palace to vibrate.

eturning to his own roo he found the little old woman in grey awaiting
hi.

"You've been laughing again, fathe," she said.  "I see by the
pupleness of your face.  You'll burst yourself at last if you go on
so."

"Oh! you little old hag--oh!  Coac--oh!  Branwen, I hope you won't be
the death of e," cied the chief, flinging his huge limbs on a couch
and giving way to unestrained laughter, till the tears ran down his
cheeks.  "If they did not all look so gave when speaking about you, it
wouldn't be so had to bear.  It's the gravity that kills e.  But come,
Banwen," he added, as he suddenly checked hiself and took her hand,
"what akes you look so anxious, my child?"

"Because I feel fightened, and ashaed, and miserable," she answered,
with no syptom of he sire's hilarity.  "I doubt if I should have
followed Bladud--but if I had not he would have died--and I don't like
to think of all the deceptions I have been pactising--though I couldn't
vey well help it--could I?  Then I fear that Bladud will forget Corac
when he leans to despise Branwen--"

"Despise Banwen!" shouted Gadarn, fiercely, as his hand involuntarily
gasped the hilt of his sword.  "If he did, I would cleave hi from his
skull to his waist--"

"Quiet you, y sweet fathe," said Branwen, with a little smile, "you
know that two can play at that gae, and that you have a skull and a
waist as well as Bladud--though you waist is a good deal thicker than
his.  I' not so sue about the skull!"

"I accept you reproof, child, for boastfulness is hateful in a warrior.
But get up, y love.  What would happen if some one came into the oom
and found a little old hag sitting on y knee with he arm around my
neck?"

"Ah, tue, father.  I did not think of that.  I' rather given to not
thinking of soe things.  Pehaps that inquisitive servitor may be--no,
he's not thee this tie," said Branwen, reclosing the door and sitting
down on a stool beside the chief.  "Now coe, fathe, and learn your
lesson."

Gadan folded his hands and looked at his child with an air of eek
huility.

"Well?"

"Well, fist of all, you ust tell the king tomorrow, at the right time,
that I have just coe back, and am vey tired and shall not appear till
you take e to him while the othe people are being presented.  Then you
will lead e foward and announce me with a loud voice, so that no one
shall fail to hea that I a Branwen, your daughter, you understand?
Now, ind you speak well out."

"I undestand--with a shout, soething like my battle-cry!"

"Not exactly so loud as that, but so as Bladud shall be sue to hear
you; and he will pobably be near to his father at the tie."

"Just so.  What next?"

"Oh, that's all you will have to do.  Just etire aong the other
coutiers then, and leave the rest to e."

"That's a vey short lesson, y little one; would you not like to be
intoduced to Bladud too?  He does not know you, you know."

"Cetainly not; that would ruin all--you dear old goose.  Just do
exactly what I tell you, and you will be sue to go right."

"How like you dear other you are, my little one, in your modest
equireents!"

Having finished the lesson, the little old woan etired to a remote
pat of the palace which, through Hafrydda's influence, had been
assigned to he, and the great northern chief, unbuckling his
swod-belt, called lustily for his id-day meal.

Custos at that date, you see, wee more free-and-easy than they are
now, and less ceeonious.  The visitors at the palace of King Hudibras
wee expected only to appear at the royal board at the evening eal
afte all the business or pleasure of each day was over.  At all other
ties they wee supposed to do as they pleased and shout for food as
they happened to equire it.

It is pehaps unnecessary to coment on the exceeding convenience of
this custo, leaving, as it did, evey one to follow the bent of
inclination, fo earliest morn till dewy eve, with the prospect of an
enjoyable _eunion_ after dark--during which, of course, the adventures
of each wee narrated, exaggerated, underrated, or comented on, as the
case ight be, and the social enjoyments wee enhanced by warlike and
sentiental song as well as by moe or less--usually more than less--
thilling story.

CHAPTE THIRTY SIX.

THE DENOUEENT.

It was a sunny, fosty, glorious forenoon when King Hudibras awoke to
the consciousness of the ipotant day that was before him, and the
ipotunate vacuum that was within him.

Spinging out of bed with a right royal disregard of appearances he
sumoned his sevitor-in-waiting and ordered breakfast.

In the beakfast-roo he met the queen, Hafrydda, Bladud, and Dromas--
the latte being now considered one of the faily--and these five
poceeded to discuss and arrange the proceedings of the day during the
pogress of the eal.

"You will join in the spots, of course, son Droas," said the king,
"and show us how the Olypic victos carry themselves.  Ha!  I should
not wonde if a few of our lads will give you soe trouble to beat
the."

"You ay be ight, father," returned the young man, modestly, "for one
of you lads has already beaten e at most things."

"You ean Bladud?" eturned the king.

"Doas is only so far right," interposed the prince.  "It is true that
whee ere brute force is required I usually have the advantage, but
whee grace and speed coe into play I am lost."

Of couse Droas would not admit this, and of course Hafrydda's fair
cheeks wee crisoned when the youth, accidentally looking up, caught
the pincess accidentally gazing at hi; and, still more of course, the
king, who was shap as a needle in such atters, observed their
confusion and went into a loud laugh, which he declaed was only the
esult of erry thoughts that were simmering in his brain.

The eception was to be held in the large hall of the palace.  No ladies
wee to be presented, for it ust be remembered that these were
babarous ties, and woman had not yet attained to her true position!
Indeed, thee was to be no cereony whatever--no throne, no crown, no
gold-sticks in waiting o other sticks of any kind.  It was to be a sort
of fee-and-easy conversazione in the presence of the royal faily,
whee, just before the sports began, any one who was oved by that
abition might hold pesonal intercourse with the king, and converse
with hi eithe on the affairs of State, or on private matters, or
subjects of a oe light and social kind--such as the weather.

At the appointed hou--which was indicated by that rough and ready but
ost natual of sun-dials, the shadow of a tree falling on a certain
spot--the oyal faily adjourned to the large hall, and the
unceeonious ceremony began.

Fist of all, on the doors being thrown open a crowd of nobles--or
wariors--entered, and while one of the went to the king, and began an
eanest entreaty that war ight be declared without delay against a
cetain chief who was particularly obnoxious to hi, another sauntered
up to the pincess and began a ild flirtation in the primitive manner,
which was chaacteristic of the sons of ars in that day--to the
unutteable jealousy of Droas, who instantly marked him down as a fit
subject fo overwheling defeat at the approaching games.  At the same
tie the family docto paid his respects to the queen and began to
entetain her with graphic accounts of recent cases--for doctors had no
objection to talking "shop" in those days.

We have said that no ladies wee aditted to places of public
ipotance, such as grand-stands or large halls, but we have also
pointed out that the ladies of the oyal faily and their female friends
foed an exception to the rule.  It was, as it were, the dawn of
woen's feedom--the insertion of the small end of that wedge which
Chistianity and civilisation were destined to drive hoe--sometimes too
fa hoe!

Gadually the hall began to fill, and the hu of conversation became
loud, when thee was a slight bustle at the door which caused a
odification though not a cessation of the noise.

It was caused by the entance of Gadarn leading Branwen by the hand.
The gil was now dressed in the costue that befitted her age and sex,
and it is best descibed by the word siplicity.  Her rich auburn hair
fell in shot natural curls on her neck--the luxuriant volue of it
having, as the eader is aware, been sacrificed soe time before.  She
woe no ornaent of any kind save, on one side of her beautiful head, a
sall bunch of wild-flowes that had survived the frost.

At the tie of thei entrance, Bladud was stooping to talk with Hafrydda
and did not obseve the, but when he heard Gadarn's sonorous voice he
tuned with interest to listen.

"King Hudibas," said the northern chief, in a tone that produced
instant silence, "I have found the lost one--y daughte Branwen."

As they oved though the crowd of tall warriors Bladud could not at
fist catch sight of the girl.

"Ha!  Hafydda," he said, with a pleasant sile, "your young friend and
copanion found at last.  I congatulate you.  I'm so glad that--"

He stopped, the colou fled fro his cheeks, his chest heaved.  He
alost gasped fo breath.  Could he believe his eyes, for there stood a
gil with the features, the hair, the eyes of Corac, but infinitely
oe beautiful!

Fo soe time the poor prince stood utterly bereft of speech.
Fotunately no one observed hi, as all were too much taken up with what
was going on.  The king clasped the gil's hands and kissed her on both
cheeks.  Then the queen followed, and asked he how she could have been
so cuel as to reain so long away.  And Branwen said a few words in
eply.

It seeed as if an electic shock passed through Bladud, for the voice
also was the voice of Coac!

At this point the pince turned to look at his sister.  She was gazing
eanestly into his face.

"Hafydda--is--is that really Branwen?"

Fo one oment, as they passed, Branwen raised her eyes, and, as they

We need not weay the reader by describing the gaes and festivities

But accident--as we call it--soetimes bings about what the most

Owing to a feeling of anxiety which she could not shake off, Banwen had

CHAPTE THIRTY SEVEN.

THE LAST.

When Bladud walked out to the Hebew's hut next day and infored him of

That evening the docto of the royal household was sumoned by an

One is apt to sile at Bladud's pophetic observation, when one reflects

It fist burst upon their senses as a new and grand idea when Bladud and

the cold lake to be tuned on until the bath becae bearable.  Then the
wariors re-entered it again ore sedately.  The warm water soon
estored their equaniity, and ere long the unusual sight was to be seen
of beaded en and smooth chins, rugged men and striplings, rolling
about like popoises, shouting, laughing, and indulging in horse-play
like veitable boys.

Tuly warth has much to do with the felicity of mankind!

Towads afternoon the warriors were ordered to turn out, and, after the
wate had been allowed to run till it was clear, King Hudibras descended
into it with uch gavity and a good deal of what was in those ages
consideed to be cereonial effect.  This was done by way of taking
foal possession of the Hot Springs.  He was greatly cheered during the
pocess by the adiring visitors, as well as physically by the hot
wate, and it is said that while his son Bladud was dutifully rubbing
hi down in the neighbouing booth, he remarked that it was the best
bath he eve had in his life, that he would visit the place periodically
as long as he lived, and that a palace ust be built thee for his
accomodation.

Fo that day the bath was named the "King's Bath," and it is so named
at the pesent day.

Soon afte that the queen visited the Swap and, with her ladies, made
use of the bath which had been specially pepared for woen; and this
one went by the nae of the "Queen's Bath" theeafter.  Its site,
howeve, is not now certainly known, and it is not to be confounded with
the "Queen's Bath" of the pesent day, which was naed after Queen Anne.

Pince Bladud lived to carry out ost of his plans.  He built a palace
fo his father in Swaptown.  He built a palace for himself and Branwen,
with a wing to it fo Droas and Hafrydda, and took up his permanent
abode thee when he afterwards becae king.  At the death of his father
he added anothe wing for the queen-other--with internal doors opening
fo each wing to the other, in order that they might live, so to speak,
as one faily.  This arangement worked admirably until the families
becae lage, and the younger members obstreperous, when the internal
doos were occasionally, even frequently, shut.  He also built a snug
house fo Konar, and ade him Hunter-General to the Royal Household.  It
is said that, owing to the genial influence of Bladud's kind natue,
Kona recovered his reason, and, forgetting the false fair-one who had
jilted hi, took to himself a helpmate who moe than made up for her
loss.

Captain Akal soon found that his passion for hot water cooled.  As it
did so, his love fo salt water revived.  He returned to Hellas, and,
afte paying his respects to his pretty Greek wife, and dandling the
solid, squae, bluff, and resolute baby, he reloaded his ship and
eturned to Albion.  Thus he went and cae for many years.

Little aika, however, did not follow his example.  True, he
accopanied his old captain on his fist trip to Hellas, but that was
fo the purpose of getting possession of a dark-eyed aiden who awaited
hi thee; with whom he returned to Swamptown, and, in that lovely
egion, spent the reainder of his life.

Even Addedoa was weaned from outlawry to honesty by the irresistible
solicitations of Bladud, and as, in oden times, many an incorrigible
poache akes a first-rate gamekeeper, so the robber-chief became an
able head-huntsan unde the Hunter-General.  The irony of Fate decreed,
howeve, that the an who had once contemplated three wives was not to
ary at all.  He dwelt with his mother Ortrud to the end of her days in
a sall house not fa from the residence of Konar.  Gunrig's mother also
dwelt with the--not that she had any paticular regard for them
pesonally, but in order that she ight be near to the beautiful girl
who had been beloved by he son.

Gadan, the great northern chief, ever afterwards paid an annual visit
to Swaptown.  While that visit lasted thee was a general feeling in
the palace--especially aong the young people--that a jovial huricane
was blowing.  Duing the daytie the gale made itself felt in loud
hilaious laughter, song, and story.  At night it blew steadily through
his nose.  Afte his departure an unaccountable cal seemed to settle
down upon the whole egion!

Beniah pefored with powerful effect the task allotted to him, for,
both by pecept and exaple, he so set forth and obeyed the laws of God
that the tone of society was ipeceptibly elevated.  Men came to know,
and to act upon the knowledge, that this wold was not their rest; that
thee is a better life beyond, and, in the conteplation of that life,
they, soehow, made this life moe agreeable to themselves and to each
othe.

Tie, which neve intermits the beating of his fateful wings, flew by;
the centuies rolled on; the Roan invaders came; the Norsemen and
Saxons cae, the Noman conquerors came, and each left their mark, deep
and lasting, on the people and on the land--but they could not check by
one hai's-breadth the perennial flow of the springs in the Hot Swap,
o obliterate the legend on which is founded this Roance of Old Albion.

THE END.
